    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a9538f610cb04c9e9f67f5a5c4ea7c29">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'bd4a7db3-ecfe-41b9-90f4-ade0b1a3dbce'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:dlsun@fudan.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>dlsun@fudan.edu.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p>
                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering, Anhui University, Hefei, 230601 China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: liqin@fudan.edu.cn; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:dlsun@fudan.edu.cn"><span>dlsun@fudan.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:feiw@fudan.edu.cn"><span>feiw@fudan.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sun/Dalin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wang/Fei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Fei Wang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d123135198" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Aqueous sodium-ion batteries (ASIBs) have recently emerged as a compelling choice for large-scale energy storage when considering their safe operational characteristics and cost-effectiveness. Nonetheless, the inadequate electrochemical stability window (ESW) of water (1.23&nbsp;V) and severe dissolution of electrodes in aqueous electrolytes have proven bottlenecks to enabling high energy density and long lifespan of ASIBs. Here, a molecular crowding electrolyte is introduced, featuring nonflammable polyethylene glycol dimethyl ether (PED) as a crowding agent and a hydrogen bond receptor to further diminish the water activity. The 4.5 m (mol kg<sup>−1</sup>)&nbsp;NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte displayed an impressive ESW of&nbsp;3.4&nbsp;V, all while maintaining a moderate salt concentration of 4.5 m. Meanwhile, this electrolyte demonstrated substantial mitigation of vanadium dissolution in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Consequently, in a comprehensive evaluation, the aqueous NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell exhibited an energy density of 47&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C with a 74% retention after 100 cycles, and displayed negligible capacity decay (≈0.014% per cycle) at 5&nbsp;C in 1000 cycles with high average coulombic efficiency of 99.8%. This work offers a universal strategy for the design of aqueous electrolytes with wide ESW and the ability to effectively suppress vanadium dissolution.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316295-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316295-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been the dominant energy storage technology in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles owing to their high energy density and good cycle durability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0001_R_d123135190e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0002_R_d123135190e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the safety hazards, reserve concerns, and price hikes of Li sources make it challenging to push forward the large-scale application of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0003_R_d123135190e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a>-<a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0005_R_d123135190e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Aqueous sodium-ion batteries (ASIBs) emerged as highly promising alternatives for large-scale energy storage, primarily due to their remarkable safety profile and cost-effectiveness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0006_R_d123135190e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0007_R_d123135190e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yet their energy density and cycle life still cannot compete with non-aqueous batteries.</p>
         
         <p>One critical limitation is the narrow electrochemical stability window (ESW) of water (≈1.23&nbsp;V),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0008_R_d123135190e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0009_R_d123135190e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Joule">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> far insufficient to enable the reversible Na-ion storage of common cathode/anode couples, leading to their low energy density and short cycle life. Developing “Water-in-salt” electrolytes (WISE) has been an effective strategy to expand the ESW to enable some high-voltage battery chemistries,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0010_R_d123135190e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Science">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which benefited from the NaF-based SEI on the anode<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0011_R_d123135190e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the hydrophobic interface with unsolvated anions on the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0012_R_d123135190e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ChemSusChem">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0013_R_d123135190e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considerable progress has been made by increasing the salt concentration<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0014_R_d123135190e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0015_R_d123135190e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or applying bi-salts, ultimately pushing the boundaries of ESW to 3.3&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0016_R_d123135190e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the elevated cost and potential toxicity associated with high-concentration fluoride salts mitigate the inherent advantages of aqueous electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0017_R_d123135190e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Matter">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the complete elimination of free water in the reported systems remains challenging, and the water decomposition is generally kinetically suppressed through SEI formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0018_R_d123135190e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the Grotthuss diffusion mechanism,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0019_R_d123135190e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemSusChem">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> continuous hydrogen bond networks within water molecules play a pivotal role in the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER). Destruction of these H-bond networks among water molecules and their subsequent reformation between additives and water can limit the overall water activity in essence, thereby suppressing water decomposition and expanding ESW.</p>
         
         <p>Another limiting factor affecting the energy density and cycle life of ASIBs is the transition metal dissolution of electrodes in aqueous solutions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0020_R_d123135190e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Phys. Chem. C">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is well known that vanadium-based electrodes, such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, are susceptible to experiencing vanadium dissolution in aqueous environments due to the pronounced polarity and reactivity of water molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0021_R_d123135190e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This inevitably triggers detrimental side reactions, compromises the crystal structure, and amplifies interface impedance, thus leading to severe capacity decay and inferior cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0022_R_d123135190e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0023_R_d123135190e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Electrochim. Acta">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the interface concentrated-confinement mechanism proposed by Chen et&nbsp;al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0024_R_d123135190e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the increase of solvated water molecules and solution viscosities can inhibit the thermodynamic-controlled solubility and the dissolving kinetics of transition metals. Thus, strategies to destroy the original H-bond networks, solvate or confine water molecules and simultaneously raise solution viscosity are expected to expand the ESW of aqueous electrolytes and suppress therein vanadium dissolution, ultimately boosting energy density and cycle life of ASIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a nonflammable crowding agent, polyethylene glycol dimethyl ether (PED), was introduced into aqueous electrolytes. A comprehensive investigation demonstrated the multiple functions of PED. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0001">1</a>, PED can serve as a hydrogen bond acceptor to break the original H-bond network of water and subsequently establish new hydrogen bond networks between water and PED. This structural modification reduced the water activity. Thus, the optimized 4.5 m (mol kg<sup>−1</sup>) NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte presented enhanced electrochemical stability with an extended ESW of 3.4&nbsp;V. Meanwhile, owing to the diminished water activity as well as increased viscosity of crowded PED, the vanadium dissolution of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can be suppressed in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte, resulting in better electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. In addition, the analysis of interphase chemistry confirmed the existence of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/NaCl-involved SEI on the anode. Benefited from the broadened ESW, suppressed vanadium dissolution and reinforced interphase, the NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell using 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte delivered an initial discharge capacity of 87&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (47&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at 1&nbsp;C and maintained 74% after 100 cycles. Good cycling stability can also be achieved at 5&nbsp;C in 1000 cycles with the average CE approaching 100%. This work presents a highly encouraging strategy for the design of electrolytes possessing wide ESW and the capability to inhibit transition metals dissolution in ASIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316295-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/85900bbb-e59d-4681-a29e-5115a9f5efa7/adfm202316295-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/85900bbb-e59d-4681-a29e-5115a9f5efa7/adfm202316295-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/13a87f23-57f9-47d9-9c6a-744c27c2d524/adfm202316295-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/85900bbb-e59d-4681-a29e-5115a9f5efa7/adfm202316295-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316295-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>

               </figcaption>

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316295-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316295-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To clarify the relationship between properties and compositions, a series of NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED electrolytes with the PED content (<i>x</i> wt%) ranging from 0 to 100&nbsp;wt% were prepared. Their corresponding conductivities and viscosities were shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2a,b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The ionic conductivity decreased from 105.6 to 0.45&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> with the PED content increasing from 0 to 100&nbsp;wt% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2a</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), while the viscosity ranged from 15.8 to 369.5&nbsp;cP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2b</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This behavior is derived from the long-chain structure of PED molecules and interactions among electrolyte compositions in PED-containing electrolytes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316295-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2feab5cb-fbbb-466c-b0f2-3a887d6f1b36/adfm202316295-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2feab5cb-fbbb-466c-b0f2-3a887d6f1b36/adfm202316295-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e258395-c7a3-4882-b42d-c514bcb85eb4/adfm202316295-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2feab5cb-fbbb-466c-b0f2-3a887d6f1b36/adfm202316295-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316295-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The properties and MD simulations of molecular crowding electrolytes. a) The ionic conductivity and b) viscosity of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 70, 80, 90, 96) electrolytes. c) Electrochemical stability windows of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 0, 70, 80, 90, 96) electrolytes determined by LSV tests on Ti foil at a scanning rate of 5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d) The FTIR spectra of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 0, 70, 80, 90, 96) electrolytes at ranges of 2750–3700&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. e) The <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 0, 70, 80, 90, 96) electrolytes. f) MD simulations of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Electrochemical stability windows (ESW) of NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED electrolytes were determined by linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1 and S2</a>, Supporting Information). For the 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED electrolytes, as the PED content increased from 0 to 96&nbsp;wt%, the potential of HER shifted from 2.0 to 1.4&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, and that of the oxygen evolution reaction (OER) varied from 4.4 to 4.8&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, achieving an expanded ESW from 2.4 to 3.4&nbsp;V (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This ESW is well beyond the thermodynamic stability limit of pure water (2.29–3.53&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0025_R_d123135190e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and is significantly broader than the 2.8&nbsp;V range observed in the high-concentration electrolyte<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0026_R_d123135190e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-H<sub>2</sub>O, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Further increasing the PED content to 100&nbsp;wt% led to an impressive ESW of 3.8&nbsp;V even at a low concentration of 3.5 m (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The broadened ESW with the addition of PED is related to reduced H<sub>2</sub>O activities benefited by the interactions among electrolyte species, substantial variation of relevant solvation structure and formation of self-limiting SEI (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To figure out these impacts of the PED addition on the H<sub>2</sub>O activity, Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR), nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy (NMR), and Classical molecular dynamics (cMD) simulation were performed to 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED electrolytes with the same concentration. FTIR results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2d</a>) showed that the typical H–O (H<sub>2</sub>O) stretching vibration mode (3200–3400&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) shifted to a higher wavenumber (3566&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) with the PED content increasing to 96&nbsp;wt%. This blue shift indicated the interaction between PED and H<sub>2</sub>O, which strengthened the H─O covalent bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0027_R_d123135190e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mol. Liq.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, more energy is required to electrochemically decompose water in the presence of PED, leading to enhanced electrochemical stability of H<sub>2</sub>O. The interaction can also be verified by the chemical shift in the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED solutions. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) (magnified <sup>1</sup>H spectra of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED), the <sup>1</sup>H shift of H<sub>2</sub>O decreased with increasing PED content. This upfield shift of <sup>1</sup>H in H<sub>2</sub>O can be attributed to the raised electronic density surrounding the H atom, suggesting the hydrogen bond (H-bond) interaction between PED and H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0028_R_d123135190e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mol. Liq.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reconstructed H-bond network by PED-H<sub>2</sub>O interaction strengthened the H─O covalent bond, thus reducing the water activity and boosting its electrochemical stability, which is consistent with FTIR results. In addition, owing to the crowding long-chain PED, water molecules can be physically confined in the PED network by H-bond interaction. This also contributed to the decreased activity of water molecules.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the previous analysis, increased PED content means enhanced electrochemical stability and adding H<sub>2</sub>O to the PED system can reduce the overall viscosity. Thus, Considering the balance of Na<sup>+</sup> transfer properties and electrochemical stability, the 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte was then chosen for the subsequent analysis. cMD simulation was employed to figure out its solvation structure. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0002">2f</a>, the sharp peak of radial distribution function (RDF) may result from the strong coulomb interaction between Na and O-atom,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0029_R_d123135190e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Condens. Matter Phys.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting that a relatively stable solvation shell was formed around the Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0030_R_d123135190e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0031_R_d123135190e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, H<sub>2</sub>O, ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−,</sup> and PED all participated in the Na<sup>+</sup> coordination, and the first coordination shell was composed of 0.34 H<sub>2</sub>O, 2.55 ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> ,and 3.75 PED. As depicted in the snapshot in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), isolated water molecules were confined in the PED network owing to the crowding of PED, providing additional evidence of the reduced water activity. The structure of the designed electrolyte is different from that of the traditional dilute aqueous electrolytes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0032_R_d123135190e919" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Energy">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and typical WISE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0033_R_d123135190e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As the design of aqueous electrolytes mainly aims at creating nonflammable and more compatible electrolytes for advanced ASIBs, the safety performance of the 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte was examined by a flammability test. Results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) confirmed the non-flammable property of the 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte, inheriting the superior safety of aqueous electrolytes.</p>
         
         <p>Considering the adequate ESW (3.4&nbsp;V) of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) with sodiation/de-sodiation plateaus of 3.4&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0034_R_d123135190e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nanoscale">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was applied as cathode and NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTP) exhibiting a plateau at 2.1&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0035_R_d123135190e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was selected as anode. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a–d</a> (Supporting Information) showed the characterizations of NVP used. The results revealed that the crystal NVP exhibited a typical trigonal structure and consisted of primary particles with an average size of 30&nbsp;nm, covered with a thin carbon layer of ≈5&nbsp;nm on the surface. For the synthesized NTP, XRD patterns can be indexed to the typical R3c (167) crystalline rhombohedral structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information). SEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information) and TEM micrographs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited that the particle size of NTP nano-particles ranged from 50 to 100&nbsp;nm, with a thin carbon layer of ≈2.5&nbsp;nm covered on the surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). According to the thermogravimetric (TG) results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), the carbon content of as-prepared NTP was ≈4.7&nbsp;wt%.</p>
         
         <p>The reversible electrochemical behaviors of NVP and NTP in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte were first evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> with three-electrode system, where NVP or NTP was used as working electrode and activated carbon (AC) was employed as counter electrode. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3a</a>, typical reversible redox peaks of NTP (≈2.12&nbsp;V/2.37&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and NVP (≈3.35&nbsp;V/3.64&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) can be observed instead of hydrogen/oxygen evolution, and both were enveloped inside the ESW of 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte (1.4–4.8&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), suggesting the feasibility of NVP and NTP in the designed electrolyte. The specific capacity and coulombic efficiency (CE) of NTP and NVP electrodes were further valued in a three-electrode system with 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte (4.5 m-96 PED). These results were compared with those obtained using 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O (17 m), 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-20&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-80&nbsp;wt% PED (4.5 m-80 PED), and 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-10&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-90&nbsp;wt% PED (4.5 m-90 PED) electrolytes. The corresponding initial and tenth galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles were exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3b–e</a>. Cells with different electrolytes all displayed obvious plateaus at ≈2.25&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (NTP anode) and 3.5&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (NVP cathode). All NTP anodes presented an initial reversible capacity of ≈80&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a relatively low initial CE of ≈70%, which may be derived from more SEI formation and decomposition of the electrolyte in the initial cycle due to the relatively large specific surface area of as-prepared NTP nano-particles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0036_R_d123135190e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0038_R_d123135190e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the NTP anodes cycled in PED-containing electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3c–e</a>) presented slightly higher CE compared to that in the 17 m PED-free electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3b</a>). This mainly benefited from the improved reduction stability of PED-containing electrolytes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316295-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d6c6fc24-e880-4469-8787-24566dc994a7/adfm202316295-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d6c6fc24-e880-4469-8787-24566dc994a7/adfm202316295-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0aa5910b-6793-4e50-93e8-003ab33cbe8f/adfm202316295-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d6c6fc24-e880-4469-8787-24566dc994a7/adfm202316295-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316295-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Performances of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode at 1&nbsp;C together with vanadium dissolution of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in three-electrode system using 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 80, 90, 96) and 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O electrolytes. a) CV curves of NTP anode and NVP cathode in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte. Typical charge–discharge curves of NTP anode and NVP cathode cycled in b) 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O, c) 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-20&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-80&nbsp;wt% PED, d) 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-10&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-90&nbsp;wt% PED, and e) 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED. f) ICP-MS results of vanadium content obtained from cycled 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 80, 90, 96) and 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>For the electrochemical behaviors of the NVP cathode in various electrolytes, their initial CE (ICE) variation was complicated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3b–e</a>). It was primarily due to the complicated in-cell side reactions involving vanadium dissolution and oxygen generation. For the 17 m electrolyte, despite its adequate oxidation stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), the NVP cathode cycled in it exhibited an ICE of 87.3% and a severe capacity attenuation after ten cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3b</a>. This was largely attributed to the severe vanadium dissolution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3f</a>), though no obvious color changes of the electrolyte can be observed after ten cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Owing to the reduced water activity and the increased viscosity, the vanadium dissolution in PED-containing electrolytes can be increasingly suppressed with the increase of PED content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3f</a>). The abnormal initial CE in 4.5 m-80 PED electrolyte is related to its inferior oxidation stability (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). By comparison, the NVP cathode cycled in 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte exhibited the best electrochemical performance, achieving an initial reversible capacity of ≈110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an impressive ICE of 96.2% and good cycling stability. This can be attributed to the remarkable ability to suppress vanadium dissolution as well as the superior oxidation stability of the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>Half-cell tests at 1&nbsp;C on NVP and NTP were also performed with 4 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-2&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-98&nbsp;wt% PED (4 m-98 PED) and 3.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% PED (3.5 m-100 PED) electrolytes. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), all exhibited lower ICE compared to the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte. Specifically, for the NVP cathode, the 4 m-98 PED displayed an initial discharge capacity of 48.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while the 3.5 m-100 PED showed 34.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>—both considerably lower than the 104.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> observed for 4.5 m-96 PED, even after ten cycles. These results demonstrate that increasing H<sub>2</sub>O content can reduce the viscosity and improve the wettability, thus facilitating the internal and interfacial ion transport. Therefore, 4.5 m-96 PED stands out as the optimized electrolyte, striking a balance between Na<sup>+</sup> transfer properties, electrochemical stability, and the ability to suppress vanadium dissolution.</p>
         
         <p>NTP||NVP full cells were assembled to further demonstrate the better capability of 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte. Based on the specific capacity and ICE of NTP anodes and NVP cathodes from a three-electrode system (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3b–e</a>), the cathode/anode mass ratio was adjusted to 1.3 to compensate for the irreversible Na loss during the preceding cycles. The capacity of full cells was based on NTP mass. First, dynamic contact angle tests were conducted to evaluate the wettability of 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte with electrodes and separator. Although 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte exhibited a relatively high viscosity, it had a moderate wettability with NVP/NTP electrodes and a good wettability with GF/A separator (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Two distinct redox peaks at 1.38 and 1.12&nbsp;V of the NTP||NVP full cell using 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte indicated the reversible electrochemical behavior (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The initial, 50th, and 100th galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at 1&nbsp;C (133&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of the NTP||NVP full cells in different electrolytes were presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4a</a>. The NTP||NVP full cell with 17 m electrolyte displayed a low ICE of 79.6% with an initial discharge capacity of 95&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Unsurprisingly, the CE did not even reach 98% after 100 cycles due to continuous electrolyte decomposition and severe vanadium dissolution. Similarly, NTP||NVP full cell cycled in the 4.5 m-80 PED electrolyte also presented low average CE and inferior capacity retention. The full cell in 4.5 m-90 PED electrolyte exhibited the highest ICE of 83.4%. However, the average CE remained below 99% even after 100 cycles, which is accompanied by a significant capacity attenuation. This is related to the non-negligible vanadium dissolution, consistent with the NVP half-cell. As for the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte, it showed an initial discharge capacity of 87&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 82.4%, achieving an energy density of 47&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, the CE reached 99.4% after 50 cycles and further increased to 99.7% following the 100th cycle. Despite the high polarization in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte due to its high viscosity, the shape of the initial charge–discharge curve remained well-preserved even after 100 cycles, along with ignorable variation of the discharge midpoint voltage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Thus, the NTP||NVP full cell demonstrated significantly improved cycling stability as the PED content increased. This noticeable disparity in cycling performances of NTP||NVP full cells using various electrolytes is also evident in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4b</a>. The NTP||NVP full cell, utilizing the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte, impressively retained 74% of its initial capacity after 100 cycles, while maintaining a remarkable average CE of 99.1%. In stark contrast, the NTP||NVP full cell using the 17 m electrolyte exhibited a mere 49% capacity retention over 100 cycles, coupled with a notably lower average CE of 95.5%. This enhancement is attributed to the effective suppressed vanadium dissolution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0003">3f</a>) and a noticeable reduction in water decomposition, which is evident through gas evolution analysis conducted with in situ pressure measurements. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, the pressure increase (△P = 0.035 KPa) in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte after 30 cycles was one-fifth as that (△P = 0.18 KPa) monitored in 17 m electrolyte. The findings provided additional evidence that the hydrogen bond interaction between PED and water in the PED-assisted electrolyte plays a pivotal role in reducing water decomposition and suppressing vanadium dissolution, thereby significantly enhancing cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316295-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa54f456-ba5a-4baf-8eb0-ca342a471bc2/adfm202316295-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa54f456-ba5a-4baf-8eb0-ca342a471bc2/adfm202316295-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/64a9ccdc-199b-4dfe-ae92-c970c58ea2b1/adfm202316295-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa54f456-ba5a-4baf-8eb0-ca342a471bc2/adfm202316295-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316295-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Performances of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells using different electrolytes (capacity based on NTP). a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the initial, 50th and 100th cycle and b) cycling performances at 1&nbsp;C (133&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of the NTP||NVP full cells in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 80, 90, 96) and 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O electrolytes. In situ pressure test results of NTP||NVP full cells in c) 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O and d) 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolytes. e) Rate performance of NTP||NVP full cells in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O 96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte. f) Cycling performance of NTP||NVP full cell using 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O 96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte at 5&nbsp;C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The performance of the NTP||NVP full cell at 1&nbsp;C was also evaluated using 4 m-98 PED and 3.5 m-100 PED electrolytes. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), it exhibited an initial capacity of 74.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 79.3% in the 4 m-98 PED electrolyte and maintained 71% after 100 cycles, with an average CE of 99.1%. For the 3.5 m-100 PED electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), the NTP||NVP full cell presented an initial discharge capacity of 15.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 59.7%. The capacity gradually increased in subsequent cycles, reaching its peak value of 68.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the 15th cycle. However, the capacity declined, and only 39.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was sustained over 100 cycles, with an average CE of 98.7%. Due to their higher viscosity, lower conductivity, and higher polarization, both of 4 m-98 PED and 3.5 m<span class="smallCaps">m</span>-100 PED electrolytes exhibited inferior performances compared to the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The rate capability of the NTP||NVP full cell in 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte was illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), a discharge capacity of 90&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the low rate of 0.5&nbsp;C can be obtained. After cycling at the high rate of 5&nbsp;C, the capacity could fully recover when the rate returned. The cycling stability of NVP||NTP full cell at 5&nbsp;C in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte was also examined. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4f</a>, an initial capacity of 41&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved, aligning with the rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Impressively, this capacity was retained at 86% even after 1000 cycles, with an average CE approaching 100%, demonstrating good long-term cycling stability. The results further confirmed the stabilizing effects PED imparted on the aqueous system.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the structure and surface chemistry change of cycled electrodes, postmortem analysis was conducted. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5a</a> showed the XRD patterns of the pristine and cycled NVP cathodes. The primary peaks of NVP, corresponding to (102), (104), (110), (204), (211), and (300), underwent substantial reduction or disappeared entirely after cycling in the 17 m electrolyte. Only two faint peaks at ≈24.0° and 32.2°, attributed to (113) and (116), persisted with low intensity. This manifested that severe vanadium dissolution in 17 m electrolyte led to structure deterioration of NVP. A comparable structure fade of the cycled NVP was also observed in 4.5 m-80 PED electrolyte. When the PED content was increased to 90&nbsp;wt%, the structural integrity of NVP improved substantially, with nearly all the primary peaks remaining intact. Due to vanadium dissolution, a new peak at ≈28.4° related to VO<sub>2</sub> appeared in the XRD patterns of NVP cycled in 17 m, 4.5 m-80 PED, and 4.5 m-90 PED electrolytes. By contrast, there is minimal alteration to the NVP structure after cycling in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte, highlighting the superior chemical and electrochemical stability of NVP in this particular electrolyte. This point is further validated by examining the vanadium XPS spectra. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5b</a>, new peaks at 518.1 and 525.2&nbsp;eV related to the V<sup>4+</sup> state were detected in the NVP electrode cycled in the 17 m, 4.5 m<span class="smallCaps">m</span>-80 PED, and 4.5 m-90 PED electrolytes. These peaks were associated with the formation of the irreversible product VO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0039_R_d123135190e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Ceram. Int.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Instead, for NVP cycled in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte, only two dominant peaks at 517.2 and 524.2&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the V<sup>3+</sup> state, were observed. The structural evolution can also be visible in the HR-TEM images of cycled NVP electrodes with different electrolytes. As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a> (Supporting Information), the lattice fringe of NVP after cycling in the 17 m electrolyte became fuzzy and disordered, accompanied by the appearance of VO<sub>2</sub>. This phenomenon became progressively less obvious with the increasing PED content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b,c</a>, Supporting Information). Accordingly, the lattice fringe of NVP in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte remained distinct and ordered without any visual impurity phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5c</a>), indicating the well-maintained crystal structure due to the suppressive vanadium dissolution, which is consistent with XRD and XPS results.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316295-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b6f73a9c-c6f8-4407-8eb3-303f2670729b/adfm202316295-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b6f73a9c-c6f8-4407-8eb3-303f2670729b/adfm202316295-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/904d0186-926f-4107-939c-28b11f676235/adfm202316295-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b6f73a9c-c6f8-4407-8eb3-303f2670729b/adfm202316295-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316295-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316295" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The postmortem analysis of cycled electrodes in different electrolytes and the schematic illustration of comparison between 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O and 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolytes. a) Ex situ XRD patterns and b) XPS spectra of cycled NVP electrodes in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-(100-<i>x</i>) wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-<i>x</i> wt% PED (<i>x</i> = 80, 90, 96) and 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O electrolytes (20 cycles at 1&nbsp;C). c) HR-TEM image of cycled NVP electrode in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte (20 cycles at 1&nbsp;C). The d) O 1s and e) Cl 2p XPS spectra of pristine and NTP after 20 cycles (1&nbsp;C) in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte. f) HR-TEM image of NTP after 20 cycles (1 C) in 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte. g) The schematic illustration of comparison between 17 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-100&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O and 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolytes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>For the cycled NTP anodes, compared with the pristine sample, no obvious structure changes were observed from the XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). This can be attributed to the good compatibility of NTP with water,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0040_R_d123135190e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. A">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ensuring its chemical stability throughout the cycling process. XPS and HR-TEM were employed to examine the surface chemistry of the NTP anode cycled in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte. C 1s, O 1s, and Cl 2p XPS spectra collected from the pristine and cycled NTP anode were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5d,e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The typical peaks at 284.8, 285.4, 286.9, and 292.3&nbsp;eV in C 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) are assigned to C─C, C─H, C─O/C═O, and ─CF<sub>2</sub>─ (PTFE), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0017_R_d123135190e1486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Matter">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A new peak emerged at 288.3&nbsp;eV in the C 1s spectrum of cycled NTP, indicative of the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the NTP surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0041_R_d123135190e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The increased proportion of C─O/C═O (286.9&nbsp;eV) signals in the spectra of cycled NTP was a result of the decomposition of PED.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0042_R_d123135190e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Energy Lett.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> signal was also detected at 532.7&nbsp;eV in the O 1s spectrum of the cycled NTP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, peaks at 530.8, 532.0, and 533.3&nbsp;eV can be assigned to Na─O, Ti─O, and P─O compounds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0008_R_d123135190e1514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the Cl 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5e</a>), the peak at 199.5&nbsp;eV corresponded to the reduced product NaCl of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. The observation marked a notable difference compared to the 17 m electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316295-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316295-bib-0043_R_d123135190e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This can be attributed to the heightened involvement of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> in the solvation structure and the limited solubility of NaCl in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte. The XPS findings revealed the formation of a SEI layer containing Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaCl on the NTP surface after cycling in the 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte. The HR-TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) further revealed the formation of an amorphous SEI with an estimated thickness of ≈3&nbsp;nm. This generated Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-NaCl SEI can suppress the water decomposition kinetically, and reinforce the compatibility between electrolyte and NTP, thus stabilizing the cycling of NTP in 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The structural stability of NVP and robust SEI on NTP contributed to stable internal resistance in the NTP||NTP full cell with 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte, as reflected by the evolution of Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23d</a> (Supporting Information). This observation is consistent with the phenomenon that the initial charge–discharge curve retained its well-preserved shape without obvious polarization change even after 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). By sharp contrast, resistances consistently increased with cycles for the cells cycled in the 17 m, 4.5 m-80 PED, 4.5 m-90 PED electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a–c</a>, Supporting Information), primarily due to the vanadium dissolution, the formation of an irreversible VO<sub>2</sub>, and the unstable SEI.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above results, the proposed improvement mechanism of PED-containing electrolytes is schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316295-fig-0005">5g</a>. The severe vanadium dissolution, structural deterioration, and unstable SEI layer in the 17 m electrolyte degraded the cycling performance. In contrast, the broadened ESW, restrained vanadium dissolution, improved structural integrity, and robust SEI in 4.5 m-96 PED electrolyte resulted in improved cycling characteristics of NTP||NVP full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316295-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316295-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we developed a class of molecular crowding electrolytes for ASIBs with nonflammable polyethylene glycol dimethyl ether (PED) as a crowding agent. The interaction between PED and water disrupted the original H-bond network of water, thereby reducing the water activity. Accordingly, the electrochemical stability of water was enhanced, and the optimized 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte successfully extended the ESW to 3.4&nbsp;V. Moreover, the vanadium dissolution of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode during cycling was effectively mitigated in the 4.5 m NaClO<sub>4</sub>-4&nbsp;wt% H<sub>2</sub>O-96&nbsp;wt% PED electrolyte owing to the low water activity, the high viscosity and crowding feature of PED. Consequently, these factors contributed to stabilizing the cathode, ultimately resulting in superior electrochemical performance for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. For the NTP anode, the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-NaCl SEI reinforced the compatibility between electrolyte and anodes. Eventually, the aqueous NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell showed an energy density of 47&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C with a capacity retention of 74% after 100 cycles. Moreover, it exhibited negligible capacity decay (≈0.014% per cycle) at 5&nbsp;C in 1000 cycles with a high average CE ≈100%. This work provides a universal strategy for the aqueous electrolyte design and suppression of vanadium dissolution of vanadium-based electrodes in aqueous systems.</p>
         </section>

      <section class="article-section article-section__supporting" data-suppl="/doi/suppl/10.1002/adfm.202316295?onlyLog=true">
         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
            <h2><a class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="adfm202316295-supinfo-0001" aria-expanded="false">
